NASHVILLE, October 15, 1913. 
To His Excellency, Ben W. Hooper, Governor of Tennessee: 
Sir: In compliance with the provisions of Section 7, Sub- 
section 12, Chapter 25, of the Acts of 1873, as amended by Chap- 
ter 153, Section 17, of the Acts of 1907, I have the honor to sub- 
mit to your Excellency the statistical report of this department 
for the scholastic year ending June 30, 19138. 
Very respectfully, 
J. W. BristerR, State Superintendent.

/ QL3STATE SUPERINTENDENTS OF PUBLIC INSTRUCTION. 
The office of State Superintendent of Public Instruction was 
created in 1835, and by an Act of the Legislature of 1843, which 
went into effect in 1844, the office was abolished. The following 
Superintendents were elected by the Legislature: 
Rosert H. McEwen, 1835-1840. 
Rosert P. Currin, 1840-1841. 
Scorr TERRY, 1841-1844. 
From 1844 to 1867 the State Treasurer was ex-officio Superin- 
tendent of Public Instruction. In 1867 the office was again 
created. The following Superintendents were elected by the 
people, and served for the respective periods shown below: - 
Joun Eaton, JRr., 1867-1869. 
A. J. Treton, 1869-1871. 
In 1870 the office was again abolished, and the duties de- 
volved upon the State Treasurer, who was made ex-officio Super- 
intendent of Public Instruction. 
‘ In 1873 the office was recreated, and the following officers, 
appointed by the Governor and confirmed by the Senate, have 
served the terms shown below: 
*J. B. KILLEBREW. . 
Joun M. FLEMING, 1873-1875. 
LEON TROUSDALE, 1875-1881. 
*W. S. Doak, 1881-1882. 
iG. S. W. Crawrorp, 1882-1883. 
Tuomas H. Paring, 1883-1887.

STATE SUPERINTENDENT'S 
INTRODUCTORY 
STATEMENT ~
-P-A-G-E-16

-P-A-G-E-17
DEPARTMENT OF PUBLIC INSTRUCTION. 
NASHVILLE, TENN., October 15, 1913: 
To His Excellency, Ben W. Hooper, Governor of Tennessee. 
In compliance with Section 7, Chapter 25, Acts of 1873, I 
herewith submit the Annual Statistical Report of the Department 
of Public Instruction for the year ending June 30, 1913. 
In view of the fact that I am about to retire from the head 
of this Department and to assume the duties of a new position, 
I desire to take advantage of this opportunity to call attention 
to some important educational matters which have been dealt 
with since my last report. . 
LEGISLATION. 
It is gratifying to note the important constructive school 
legislation which was enacted by the Fifth-eighth General Assem- 
bly. The educational leaders of the State, in conference with the 
State Superintendent of Public Instruction, had prepared a gen- 
eral scheme of legislation previous to the meeting of the General 
Assembly, and several of the bills prepared, of far-reaching 
importance, were enacted into law. 
1. A law authorizing County Boards of Education to con- 
solidate schools, to provide public transportation and to elect 
supervisors of special and general school work. 
2. Anamendment to the General Educational Bill increasing 
the State School Fund from 25 to 33% per cent of the gross 
revenues of the State. 
It should be understood that this law is something more than 
a mere revenue bill. Those who have closely examined it pro- 
nounce it a splendid piece of constructive legislation. Not only 
does it provide more revenue, but it enables the State school 
authorities to project many new school activities and to give 
direction thereto. It places at the disposal of the State Board 
of Education certain funds for assisting in the construction of
-P-A-G-E-18
— 18 — 
buildings for consolidated schools, providing for public transpor- 
tation, and supplementing the salaries of county supervisors. 
This amendment, together with the first bill mentioned above, 
has already resulted in the building of many first-class consol- 
idated schools in many counties of the State and has put into 
use a number of splendidly constructed school wagons. The 
new schools have been built on desirable lots of from four to six 
acres and according to plans approved by the State Superinten- 
dent of Public Instruction. In their construction the principles 
of school sanitation and hygiene have been observed, and it is 
believed that a new standard of school building has thereby 
been set up in many counties. 
The State Board has also guaranteed assistance to. supervisors 
in a number of counties; more than twenty counties have 
appointed supervisors during the last few months—some of them 
having oversight of general school work, some supervising 
instruction in special branches, and others organizing industrial 
“work in country schools. ° . 
The work of supervision in our country schools is one of prime 
importance, second only to the teaching force. The need. of 
competent supervision is everywhere recognized, since it is 
understood that an adequate organization of a county school 
system is impossible without it. . We are making a beginning of it 
in Tennessee for the first time this year. It should go on until 
a number of supervisors, perhaps one for each school district, 
have been provided.. It is impossible in this brief resume of 
conditions to go into details concerning the work which super- 
visors must do. Suffice it to say that those already appointed 
are daily discovering new avenues of service. One of the great 
opportunities offered the State Normal Schools is to assist in 
the education and training of supervisors, and one of the big 
tasks before the State Department of Education is to discover 
and to set forth in systematic manner the duties and qualifica- 
tions of these new officials. 
This same amendment makes more generous provision for 
school library work. A director of public school libraries has 
been appointed, a revised list of books suitable for public schools 
has been published, and I am glad to report that there has been 
more activity in the establishment of school libraries during the 
.
-P-A-G-E-19
—19 — 
last four months than during any similar period since my incum- 
bency of this office. . 
3. The Compulsory School Attendance Law. The mere pas- 
sage of this law is a splendid indication of a growing sentiment 
in favor of the public school system of the State. A few years 
ago it could not have been done, and there were some who ques- 
tioned the propriety of asking its passage by the Fifty-eighth 
General Assembly; but it was introduced early in the session 
and passed with practical unanimity, and is one of the few 
general school laws ever enacted in Tennessee without any 
county exemptions. 
It has met with general favor over the State. Reports from 
many county superintendents state that it is working wonders 
in various sections. If there is no relaxation on the part of county 
school officials and the present school interest continues during 
the current year, it is safe to estimate that the attendance at 
our public schools this year will exceed any previous record by 
15 or 20 per cent. 
This Department has done every thing possible to give pub- 
licity to the law; thousands of posters were published-and dis- 
tributed to county superintendents to circulate, and thousands 
of post cards were put into the hands of teachers to solicit the 
help and cooperation of parents. It is believed that where this 
advertising material was wisely used the largest and best results 
will follow. 
It is not to be supposed that all the children of school age 
will be brought into school this year, because, unfortunately, all 
school officials are not alive to their duties; but in those counties 
where the Superintendent, members of the Board of Education, 
and teachers are concerned about the law and anxious to bring 
public school advantages within the reach of all the children of 
all the people, its benefits are being immediately felt. 
Every Tennessean ought to be ashamed of the amount of 
illiteracy which exists in the State. Its existence is a serious 
reflection on our public schools. It is true that the per- 
centage of illiteracy has been considerably reduced during 
the last ten years, but there still remains a tremendous burden 
resting upon the elementary schools of the State. The severest 
test to which they have been put is involved in this new law,
-P-A-G-E-20
—20 — 
and we have done what we could to make the school people of the 
various counties realize their responsibility. If every man and 
woman among them responds we can banish illiteracy from the 
State within the next few years, or at least reduce it to a neglible 
quantity; and if our schools accomplish this they will be well 
worthy all the financial and moral support accorded them by 
the people of the State. 
4. Uniform Examination and Certification of Teachers. In 
the development of a public school system a competent teaching 
force is always the first consideration. One of the most impor- 
tant factors in the securing of a competent teaching force is the 
establishment of a proper standard of qualification. Under 
existing conditions there are ninety-six different standards in 
the State. Under the new law which will become effective 
July 1, 1914, one standard will be set up for the whole State. 
My successor in office will have a big job on his hands in working 
out the details of this law and putting it into successful opera-~ 
tion; at the same time he has a fine opportunity, and the school 
men of the State are agreed that the benefits of the new law will 
be immediately recognized. 
These four laws, I repeat, will be of inestimable value to the 
public schools of Tennessee and their passage reflects credit on 
the law-making powers of the State. So far as I know, never 
before have so many important school bills been passed in one 
legislative session. Already the schools are feeling the effect 
of these laws, new activities are evidenced in the schools of every 
section of the State and the statistics for the current year, I feel 
safe in predicting, will demonstrate their value and importance. 
_ EVIDENCES OF SCHOOL PROGRESS. 
As already several times intimated, the public school situa- 
tion. is encouraging and there are many evidences of school 
progress. We can not set forth in figures or phrases the finer 
phases of school development, that which is expressed in the 
lives of our “boys and girls and which affects their homes and 
communities in ways not readily seen; but the growing senti- 
ment is frequently evidenced in increased revenues, in better 
houses and the enlargement of the educational machinery.
-P-A-G-E-21
Not every such improvement is reported to our office, but I 
may mention here some of those which have come to our atten- 
_tion during the last few months; that is, since January 1, 1913.

SUMMARIES .
-P-A-G-E-24

-P-A-G-E-25
— 25 — 
SUMMARIES. 
The summaries of the various statistical tables show a rea- 
sonable improvement in our school situation. - The scholastic 
population is considerably in excess of what was reported the 
previous year. A notable increase is in the number of children 
between the ages of six and twenty-one who can read and write, 
there being approximately 62,000 more than was reported in 
1912. I do not know how much credence can be put in these 
figures, but it is believed that they indicate a healthy advance. 
The school enrollment and the attendance show respectively 
an increase of 5,000 and 6,000 over the previous year. This 
increase is not large, but it shouid be borne in mind that the com- 
pulsory school law did not become effective until after the period 
for which this report is made. -It is safe to predict that the en- 
rollment for the current year, feeling the effect of the said law, 
will be greatly in excess of these figures. 
I regret to state that the length of school term in the county 
elementary schools is the same as for the previous year. Here 
is one of our greatest educational problems—that is, to bring 
about a sentiment in the various counties which will result in a 
longer school term. It has been too long felt that a four or 
five month’s term for the children of many counties is sufficient, 
while those who live in cities have the opportunity of attending 
school nine and ten months. The sooner we realize the impos- 
sibility of educating the country children with such short terms, 
the better it will be for all concerned; and I know of nothing 
better that the school people of the State could do than to use 
every effort to create this sentiment. 
The financial statistics as embodied in this report are quite 
satisfactory. The school property for 1913 totals $1,300,000 
more than was reported in 1912, and nearly a million dollars was 
“spent in the erection of school houses during the year. The 
total school receipts were nearly $7,000,000—more than $300,000 
in excess of the report of 1912. It should be understood, too, that 
this increase in revenue is not due to the amendment to the gen- 
eral education bill which increased the school fund from twenty-
-P-A-G-E-26
— 26 — 
five per cent to thirty-three and a third per cent of its gross 
revenues; the increased revenues which we are now receiving 
from the State according to this law, do not affect the receipts 
and expenditures of the year for which this report was made. 
: On the whole I am glad to report that the school situation 
is encouraging, and while improvement is slow, it is sure, and we 
have every reason to congratulate ourselves on the progress 
already made and to count on much more rapid advancement 
during the next few years. : 
Permit me, Governor, in conclusion to express to you my 
hearty appreciation of your uniform kindness and consideration. 
I have endeavored to administer the department of Public In- 
struction for the best interests of the State, and thereby to. 
justify my appointment at your hands. I have felt at all 
times that I had your sympathy and support and it has been 
one of the great pleasures of my life to be connected with your 
administration. The school interests of the State have had in 
you a consistent friend and the school people generally recognize 
their obligations to you. 
Very respectfully, 
J. W. BRIsTER.

-P-A-G-E-179
HIGH SCHOOLS
-P-A-G-E-180
Fourth Annual Report of the State High School 
Inspector for the Year Ending June 30, 1913. 
GENERAL GAINS. 
Since the last general report the following gains have been made: 
The number of high schools has increased from...........++ III to 117 
The number of teachers from... --351 to 392 
The average term from........ 173 to 175 
The salary of teachers from. . -$80,60 a month to $86.52 
The enrollment from..........cc sce e cece eee reece eee eee 7,546 to 8,310 
The average daily attendance from...... 5,495 to 6,341 
The number of high school buildings from.............. «+74 to 92 
The value of high school property from.... $996,750 to $1,152,315.25 
Receipts from all sources from........ . -$359,070 to $365,874.81 
Expenditures from .. . $318,177 to $387,676.04 
Number of books in high school libraries from............ 14,384 to’ 19,079 
     
         
      
    
         
    
DISTRIBUTION OF County HiGH ScHOoOoLs, 
Of the thirty-four counties in East Tennessee, twenty-two 
‘have county high schools. Of the forty-one counties in Middle 
Tennessee, only fourteen have high schools ; and of the twenty-one 
counties in West Tennessee, only nine have county high schools. 
Of all the high school counties in the State, forty-five, East 
Tennessee has 50 per cent, Middle Tennessee has 30 per cent, 
and West Tennessee has 20 per cent. 
Of the one hundred and seventeen high schools in the State, 
East Tennessee has fifty-four, or 48 per cent; Middle Tennessee 
has twenty-seven, or 25 per cent; and West Tennessee has thirty, 
or 27 per cent. 
In the different sections there are different conditions, that, to 
some extent, explain these differences. However, the conditions 
are now changing and there will be considerable development in 
both Middle and West Tennessee.
-P-A-G-E-181
— 183 — 
UNDESIRABLE TENDENCY. 
There is but one undesirable tendency in the development 
of our high school system. Some counties are trying to run 
too many separate schools, more than the funds provided can 
run successfully. Many counties, under the maximum tax levy 
allowed by law, are able to run but one high school. No county 
should undertake the run more than the funds provided by the 
State and county can run well. In many counties there is needed 
consolidation, even if pupils from some communities should have 
to be transported, in order that these counties may have a higher 
standard of work. Each county should have one four-year high 
school doing all the work, and then, as many two or three-year 
high schools as the funds will permit. 
THE SECONDARY SCHOOL. 
The so-called secondary schools were established at the ex- 
pense of the elementary schools of the State, and the good re- 
sults of these schools have never been commensurate with the 
cost in money and time taken from the primary grades. There 
are nine subjects to be taught in the primary, or elementary 
schools. The secondary school added eight more subjects to 
the course without providing one cent additional to take care 
of these extra subjects. In the secondary schools of the State 
the number enrolled in the secondary subjects is only one and 
one-half per cent of the total enrollment in the rural districts, 
but in the secondary schools at least one-half of the time of the 
teacher or teachers is required to teach the eight subjects of 
the secondary course; hence it is easy to see that the few in 
the secondary department are taught at a great sacrifice of time 
and money to the children in the lower grades. 
Besides, the secondary school does not atriculate with college, 
neither does it give any definite vocational advantages. The 
high school does articulate with the elementary school and with 
college, and it does give definite vocational training that counts 
for something to those pupils who cannot go to college, and it is 
not run at the expense of the children in the lower grades, for 
no county can establish a high school without providing funds to 
take care of it.
-P-A-G-E-182
— 184 — 
Hicw Scoot TEACHERS. 
A large number of the teachers employed in the high schools 
are college graduates. All teachers are licensed under rigid 
regulations of the State Board of Education. Those who do not 
meet the requirements as college graduates must take an exami- 
nation under the direction of the State Board of Education in 
order to receive either a county or a State certificate to teach. The 
interest of the people as well as the interest of the qualified teach- 
ers is thus well protected. During the past two years, since these 
requirements were made, there has not come to this department a 
single complaint that any teacher, regularly licensed, is incompe- 
tent. I do not believe that any State has a better class of high 
school teachers, and, where the local conditions will permit, I do 
not believe that better work is done. 
Courses oF Stupy, 
During the three years of their use the various courses of 
study have worked well, but present conditions and. the present 
state of high school construction make it necessary to revise 
the vocational courses. The course in Agriculture will be care- 
fully revised. A course in home economics will be worked out. 
The commercial course will be modified somewhat. A course 
for elementary teachers will be prepared and one planned for 
manual training. This revision will be made in the early spring— 
in time for use next September. 
Hicu ScHoot Tax Levy. 
In the early history of the “free” schools of the State, many 
counties would not levy a school tax. The State, realizing that 
it could not become the great State possible to be without edu- 
cating all of its citizenship, had to enact a law requiring each 
county to levy not less than 15 cents on the hundred dollars of 
taxable property for “free” schools. The wisdom of that act 
has never been questioned, and no progressive citizen would 
now oppose it. Another act that would do nearly as much good 
is one that would fix the minimum high school rate to be levied by 
each county and require each county to establish high schools for
-P-A-G-E-183
— 185 — 
» its children. Of course, the minimum should be fixed according 
to the population of the county—not the same for every county, 
for a rate for Hancock County would produce entirely too much 
for Shelby. Then, too, it seems unfair for the State to fix the 
maximum tax a county may levy, but should allow any county 
the privilege of levying whatever rate the people of that county 
want and thus give more nearly equal opportunity to all the 
children of the State. 
HiewH ScHoor Surptus. 
Under the law of 1909 the surplus of the high school fund at 
the end of the scholastic year has reverted to the common school 
fund and been distributed among the counties according to the 
scholastic population. This surplus is used under the 1913 law 
to aid the departments of agriculture and home economics in the 
high schools and will be used in this way until there are enough 
counties to establish high schools to take all the fund as now dis- 
tributed. This use will stimulate the work in these departments 
so that the counties will be willing to take proper care of them 
in the future when the surplus is no longer available. 
The law governing this act is as follows and found in Chapter 
23, Acts of 1913: 
SecTIon 3. Be it further enacted, That Section 5, Chapter 
264, Acts of 1909, be amended by striking out the last part of said 
Section 5 beginning with the words “shall revert,” and substi- 
tuting therefor the following words: “shall be used under the 
direction of the State Board of Education for the purpose of 
stimulating and encouraging the establishment and maintenance 
of departments of industrial work, including agriculture, home 
economics, mazual training and kindred subjects, and also for 
the purpose of equalizing the high schools of the various coun- 
ties; provided, that if in any county levying a tax for high school 
purposes at the highest rate permitted by law for that purpose, 
the proceeds of said levy do not amount to as much as Two 
Thousand ($2,000.00) Dollars in any year, the State Board of 
Education shall apportion to the high’ school fund of said county 
out of the State high school fund provided in Section 5, Chapter
-P-A-G-E-184
— 186 — 
264, Acts of 1909, the amount necessary to make a high school 
fund of Two Thousand ($2,000.00) Dollars for said county for 
said year.” 
INcREASED FUNDS. 
The revenues of the high school fund are increased by the new 
law of 1913, which provides that the State school fund shall 
receive 33 I-3 per cent of the gross revenues of the State instead 
of 25 per cent as formerly. The high school receives 8 per cent of 
this fund and is therefore proportionately increased. The law 
is written in Chapter 23, Acts of 1913. 
Very truly, 
Wm. R. Bourne, High School Inspector.
-P-A-G-E-185
